      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Nations_involvement">United Nations involvement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Madagascar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: United Nations involvement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>Madagascar became a Member State of the United Nations on 20 September 1960, shortly after gaining its independence on 26 June 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> As of January 2017, 34 police officers from Madagascar are deployed in Haiti as part of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Stabilisation_Mission_in_Haiti" title="United Nations Stabilisation Mission in Haiti">United Nations Stabilisation Mission in Haiti</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Starting in 2015, under the direction of and with assistance from the UN, the World Food Programme started the Madagascar Country Programme with the two main goals of long-term development and reconstruction efforts, and addressing the food insecurity issues in the southern regions of Madagascar.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-154">[154]</a></sup> These goals plan to be accomplished by providing meals for specific schools in rural and urban priority areas and by developing national school feeding policies to increase consistency of nourishment throughout the country. Small and local farmers have also been assisted in increasing both the quantity and quality of their production, as well as improving their crop yield in unfavorable weather conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-154">[154]</a></sup> In 2017, Madagascar signed the UN <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Prohibition_of_Nuclear_Weapons" title="Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons">treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Madagascar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Madagascar" title="Economy of Madagascar">Economy of Madagascar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Madagascar" title="Tourism in Madagascar">Tourism in Madagascar</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Madagascar_treemap.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Madagascar_treemap.png/350px-Madagascar_treemap.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Madagascar_treemap.png/525px-Madagascar_treemap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Madagascar_treemap.png/700px-Madagascar_treemap.png 2x" data-file-width="1107" data-file-height="866"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Madagascar_treemap.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A proportional representation of Madagascar's exports
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>During the era of Madagascar's First Republic, France heavily influenced Madagascar's economic planning and policy and served as its key trading partner. Key products were cultivated and distributed nationally through producers' and consumers' cooperatives. Government initiatives such as a rural development program and state farms were established to boost production of commodities such as rice, coffee, cattle, silk and palm oil. Popular dissatisfaction over these policies was a key factor in launching the socialist-Marxist Second Republic, in which the formerly private bank and insurance industries were nationalized; state monopolies were established for such industries as textiles, cotton and power; and importâexport trade and shipping were brought under state control. Madagascar's economy quickly deteriorated as exports fell, industrial production dropped by 75&nbsp;percent, inflation spiked and government debt increased; the rural population was soon reduced to living at subsistence levels. Over 50&nbsp;percent of the nation's export revenue was spent on debt servicing.<sup id="cite_ref-EBLand_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBLand-28">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The IMF forced Madagascar's government to accept structural adjustment policies and liberalization of the economy when the state became bankrupt in 1982 and state-controlled industries were gradually privatized over the course of the 1980s. The political crisis of 1991 led to the suspension of IMF and World Bank assistance. Conditions for the resumption of aid were not met under Zafy, who tried unsuccessfully to attract other forms of revenue for the State before aid was once again resumed under the interim government established upon Zafy's impeachment. The IMF agreed to write off half Madagascar's debt in 2004 under the Ravalomanana administration. Having met a set of stringent economic, governance and human rights criteria, Madagascar became the first country to benefit from the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Challenge_Account" class="mw-redirect" title="Millennium Challenge Account">Millennium Challenge Account</a> in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-BGNote_23-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGNote-23">[23]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Nosy-iranja-beach.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Nosy-iranja-beach.jpg/220px-Nosy-iranja-beach.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Nosy-iranja-beach.jpg/330px-Nosy-iranja-beach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Nosy-iranja-beach.jpg/440px-Nosy-iranja-beach.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2120" data-file-height="1468"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Nosy-iranja-beach.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nosy_Iranja&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nosy Iranja (page does not exist)">Nosy Iranja</a> is one of the international tourism destinations in Madagascar
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Madagascar's GDP in 2015 was estimated at US$9.98&nbsp;billion, with a per capita GDP of $411.82.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> Approximately 69&nbsp;percent of the population lives below the national poverty line threshold of one dollar per day.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> During 2011â15, the average growth rate was 2.6% but was expected to have reached 4.1% in 2016, due to public works programs and a growth of the service sector.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> The agriculture sector constituted 29&nbsp;percent of Malagasy GDP in 2011, while manufacturing formed 15&nbsp;percent of GDP. Madagascar's other sources of growth are tourism, agriculture and the extractive industries.<sup id="cite_ref-AEO2011_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEO2011-160">[160]</a></sup> Tourism focuses on the niche eco-tourism market, capitalizing on Madagascar's unique biodiversity, unspoiled natural habitats, national parks and lemur species.<sup id="cite_ref-tourismsector_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tourismsector-161">[161]</a></sup> An estimated 365,000 tourists visited Madagascar in 2008, but the sector declined during the political crisis with 180,000 tourists visiting in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-AEO2011_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEO2011-160">[160]</a></sup> However, the sector has been growing steadily for a few years; In 2016, 293,000 tourists landed in the African island with an increase of 20% compared to 2015; For 2017 the country has the goal of reaching 366,000 visitors, while for 2018 government estimates are expected to reach 500,000 annual tourists.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The island is still a very poor country in 2018; structural brakes remain in the development of the economy: corruption and the shackles of the public administration, lack of legal certainty, and backwardness of land legislation. The economy, however, has been growing since 2011, with GDP growth exceeding 4% per year;<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> almost all economic indicators are growing, the GDP per capita was around $1600 (PPP) for 2017,<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> one of the lowest in the world, although growing since 2012; unemployment was also cut, which in 2016 was equal to 2.1%<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> with a work force of 13.4&nbsp;million as of 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The main economic resources of Madagascar are <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Madagascar" title="Tourism in Madagascar">tourism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_Madagascar" title="Agriculture in Madagascar">agriculture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mining_industry_of_Madagascar" title="Mining industry of Madagascar">mining</a>. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_resources_and_trade">Natural resources and trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Madagascar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Natural resources and trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Raffia_animals_created_by_artisans_in_Madagascar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Raffia_animals_created_by_artisans_in_Madagascar.jpg/220px-Raffia_animals_created_by_artisans_in_Madagascar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Raffia_animals_created_by_artisans_in_Madagascar.jpg/330px-Raffia_animals_created_by_artisans_in_Madagascar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Raffia_animals_created_by_artisans_in_Madagascar.jpg/440px-Raffia_animals_created_by_artisans_in_Madagascar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Raffia_animals_created_by_artisans_in_Madagascar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Toy animals made from <a href="/wiki/Raffia" class="mw-redirect" title="Raffia">raffia</a>, a native palm<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Madagascar's natural resources include a variety of agricultural and mineral products. Agriculture (including the growing of <a href="/wiki/Raffia" class="mw-redirect" title="Raffia">raffia</a>), mining, fishing and <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">forestry</a> are mainstays of the economy. In 2017 the top exports were vanilla (US$894M), nickel metal (US$414M), cloves (US$288M), knitted sweaters (US$184M) and cobalt (US$143M).<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Madagascar is the world's principal supplier of <a href="/wiki/Vanilla" title="Vanilla">vanilla</a>, cloves<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ylang-ylang" class="mw-redirect" title="Ylang-ylang">ylang-ylang</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gafilo_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gafilo-30">[30]</a></sup> The island supplies 80% of the world's natural vanilla.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Other key agricultural resources include coffee, <a href="/wiki/Lychee" title="Lychee">lychees</a> and shrimp. Key mineral resources include various types of precious and semi-precious stones, and it currently provides half of the world's supply of sapphires, which were discovered near <a href="/wiki/Ilakaka" title="Ilakaka">Ilakaka</a> in the late 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Madagascar has one of the world's largest reserves of <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">ilmenite</a> (titanium ore), as well as important reserves of chromite, coal, iron, cobalt, copper and nickel.<sup id="cite_ref-EBLand_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBLand-28">[28]</a></sup> Several major projects are underway in the mining, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">gas</a> sectors that are anticipated to give a significant boost to the Malagasy economy. These include such projects as ilmenite and zircon mining from heavy mineral sands near <a href="/wiki/T%C3%B4lanaro" title="TÃ´lanaro">TÃ´lanaro</a> by <a href="/wiki/Rio_Tinto_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Rio Tinto Group">Rio Tinto</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-RioTinto_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RioTinto-173">[173]</a></sup> extraction of nickel near <a href="/wiki/Moramanga" title="Moramanga">Moramanga</a> and its processing near Toamasina by <a href="/wiki/Sherritt_International" title="Sherritt International">Sherritt International</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> and the development of the giant onshore <a href="/wiki/Heavy_crude_oil" title="Heavy crude oil">heavy oil</a> deposits at <a href="/wiki/Tsimiroro" title="Tsimiroro">Tsimiroro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bemolanga" title="Bemolanga">Bemolanga</a> by <a href="/wiki/Madagascar_Oil" title="Madagascar Oil">Madagascar Oil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Exports formed 28&nbsp;percent of GDP in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-BGNote_23-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGNote-23">[23]</a></sup> Most of the country's export revenue is derived from the textiles industry, fish and shellfish, vanilla, cloves and other foodstuffs.<sup id="cite_ref-AEO2011_160-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEO2011-160">[160]</a></sup> France is the nation's main trading partner, although the United States, Japan and Germany also have strong economic ties.<sup id="cite_ref-EBLand_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBLand-28">[28]</a></sup> The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Madagascar-U.S._Business_Council&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Madagascar-U.S. Business Council (page does not exist)">Madagascar-U.S. Business Council</a> was formed in May 2003, as a collaboration between <a href="/wiki/USAID" class="mw-redirect" title="USAID">USAID</a> and Malagasy artisan producers to support the export of local handicrafts to foreign markets.<sup id="cite_ref-handicrafts_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-handicrafts-176">[176]</a></sup> Imports of such items as foodstuffs, fuel, capital goods, vehicles, consumer goods and electronics consume an estimated 52&nbsp;percent of GDP. The main sources of Madagascar's imports include <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93Madagascar_relations" title="ChinaâMadagascar relations">China</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> France, Iran, Mauritius and Hong Kong.<sup id="cite_ref-BGNote_23-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGNote-23">[23]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure_and_media">Infrastructure and media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Madagascar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Infrastructure and media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Madagascar" title="Transport in Madagascar">Transport in Madagascar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Madagascar" title="Telecommunications in Madagascar">Telecommunications in Madagascar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_Madagascar" title="List of newspapers in Madagascar">List of newspapers in Madagascar</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Antananarivo_Madagascar_people_reading_news.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Antananarivo_Madagascar_people_reading_news.JPG/220px-Antananarivo_Madagascar_people_reading_news.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Antananarivo_Madagascar_people_reading_news.JPG/330px-Antananarivo_Madagascar_people_reading_news.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Antananarivo_Madagascar_people_reading_news.JPG/440px-Antananarivo_Madagascar_people_reading_news.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Antananarivo_Madagascar_people_reading_news.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A news stand in Antananarivo
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 2010, Madagascar had approximately 7,617&nbsp;km (4,730&nbsp;mi) of paved roads, 854&nbsp;km (530&nbsp;mi) of railways and 432&nbsp;km (270&nbsp;mi) of navigable waterways.<sup id="cite_ref-Bradtp2_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradtp2-13">[13]</a></sup> The majority of roads in Madagascar are unpaved, with many becoming impassable in the rainy season. Largely paved national routes connect the six largest regional towns to Antananarivo, with minor paved and unpaved routes providing access to other population centers in each district.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_29-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-29">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>There are several rail lines. Antananarivo is connected to Toamasina, Ambatondrazaka and Antsirabe by rail, and another rail line connects Fianarantsoa to Manakara. The most important seaport in Madagascar is located on the east coast at Toamasina. Ports at Mahajanga and Antsiranana are significantly less used because of their remoteness.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_29-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-29">[29]</a></sup> The island's newest port at Ehoala, constructed in 2008 and privately managed by Rio Tinto, will come under state control upon completion of the company's mining project near TÃ´lanaro around 2038.<sup id="cite_ref-RioTinto_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RioTinto-173">[173]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Air_Madagascar" title="Air Madagascar">Air Madagascar</a> services the island's many small regional airports, which offer the only practical means of access to many of the more remote regions during rainy season road washouts.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_29-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-29">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Running water and electricity are supplied at the national level by a government service provider, <a href="/wiki/Jirama" title="Jirama">Jirama</a>, which is unable to service the entire population. As of 2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Madagascar&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, only 6.8&nbsp;percent of Madagascar's <i>fokontany</i> had access to water provided by Jirama, while 9.5&nbsp;percent had access to its electricity services.<sup id="cite_ref-INSTAT_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INSTAT-148">[148]</a></sup> Fifty-six percent of Madagascar's power is provided by hydroelectric power plants, with the remaining 44% provided by diesel engine generators.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Mobile telephone and internet access are widespread in urban areas but remain limited in rural parts of the island. Approximately 30% of the districts are able to access the nations' several private telecommunications networks via mobile telephones or land lines.<sup id="cite_ref-INSTAT_148-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INSTAT-148">[148]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Radio broadcasts remain the principal means by which the Malagasy population access international, national, and local news. Only state radio broadcasts are transmitted across the entire island. Hundreds of public and private stations with local or regional range provide alternatives to state broadcasting.<sup id="cite_ref-mediainfo_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mediainfo-132">[132]</a></sup> In addition to the state television channel, a variety of privately owned television stations broadcast local and international programming throughout Madagascar. Several media outlets are owned by political partisans or politicians themselves, including the media groups MBS (owned by Ravalomanana) and Viva (owned by Rajoelina),<sup id="cite_ref-CrossroadsMarcus_68-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CrossroadsMarcus-68">[68]</a></sup> contributing to political polarization in reporting. </p>
      <p>The media have historically come under varying degrees of pressure to censor their criticism of the government. Reporters are occasionally threatened or harassed, and media outlets are periodically forced to close.<sup id="cite_ref-mediainfo_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mediainfo-132">[132]</a></sup> Accusations of media censorship have increased since 2009 because of the alleged intensification of restrictions on political criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-DOS_139-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DOS-139">[139]</a></sup> Access to the internet has grown dramatically over the past decade, with an estimated 352,000 residents of Madagascar accessing the internet from home or in one of the nation's many internet cafÃ©s in December 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-mediainfo_132-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mediainfo-132">[132]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Madagascar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_Madagascar" title="Healthcare in Madagascar">Healthcare in Madagascar</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Madagascar_woman_with_child.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Madagascar_woman_with_child.jpg/170px-Madagascar_woman_with_child.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Madagascar_woman_with_child.jpg/255px-Madagascar_woman_with_child.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Madagascar_woman_with_child.jpg/340px-Madagascar_woman_with_child.jpg 2x" data-file-width="683" data-file-height="1024"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Madagascar_woman_with_child.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Maternal mortality declined after 1990 but rose sharply after 2009 because of political instability.<sup id="cite_ref-INSTAT_148-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INSTAT-148">[148]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Medical centers, dispensaries and hospitals are found throughout the island, although they are concentrated in urban areas and particularly in Antananarivo. Access to medical care remains beyond the reach of many Malagasy, especially in the rural areas, and many recourse to traditional healers.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> In addition to the high expense of medical care relative to the average Malagasy income, the prevalence of trained medical professionals remains extremely low. In 2010, Madagascar had an average of three hospital beds per 10,000 people and a total of 3,150 doctors, 5,661 nurses, 385 community health workers, 175 pharmacists, and 57 dentists for a population of 22&nbsp;million. Fifteen percent of government spending in 2008 was directed toward the health sector. Approximately 70 percent of spending on health was contributed by the government, while 30 percent originated with international donors and other private sources.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2011_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-180">[180]</a></sup> The government provides at least one basic health center per commune. Private health centers are concentrated within urban areas and particularly those of the central highlands.<sup id="cite_ref-INSTAT_148-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INSTAT-148">[148]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Despite these barriers to access, health services have shown a trend toward improvement over the past twenty years. Child immunizations against such diseases as <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_B" title="Hepatitis B">hepatitis B</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a> increased an average of 60 percent in this period, indicating low but increasing availability of basic medical services and treatments. The Malagasy fertility rate in 2009 was 4.6 children per woman, declining from 6.3 in 1990. Teen pregnancy rates of 14.8 percent in 2011, much higher than the African average, are a contributing factor to rapid population growth.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2011_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-180">[180]</a></sup> In 2010, the maternal mortality rate was 440 per 100,000 births, compared to 373.1 in 2008 and 484.4 in 1990, indicating a decline in perinatal care following the 2009 coup. The infant mortality rate in 2011 was 41 per 1,000 births,<sup id="cite_ref-BGNote_23-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGNote-23">[23]</a></sup> with an under-five mortality rate at 61 per 1,000 births.<sup id="cite_ref-SOWMY_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SOWMY-181">[181]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Schistosomiasis" title="Schistosomiasis">Schistosomiasis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, and sexually transmitted diseases are common in Madagascar, although infection rates of <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">AIDS</a> remain low relative to many countries in mainland Africa, at 0.2 percent of the adult population. The malaria mortality rate is also among the lowest in Africa at 8.5 deaths per 100,000 people, in part because of the highest frequency use of insecticide treated nets in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2011_180-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-180">[180]</a></sup> Adult life expectancy in 2009 was 63 years for men and 67 years for women.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2011_180-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2011-180">[180]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Madagascar had <a href="/wiki/21st_century_Madagascar_plague_outbreaks" title="21st century Madagascar plague outbreaks">outbreaks</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pneumonic_plague" title="Pneumonic plague">pneumonic plague</a> in 2017 (2575 cases, 221 deaths) and 2014 (263 confirmed cases, 71 deaths).<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> In 2019, Madagascar had a <a href="/wiki/2018_Madagascar_measles_outbreak" title="2018 Madagascar measles outbreak">measles outbreak</a>, resulting in 118,000 cases and 1,688 deaths. In 2020, Madagascar was also affected by the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic_in_Madagascar" title="COVID-19 pandemic in Madagascar">COVID-19 pandemic</a>. Undernourishment and hunger rates were at 42% in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Madagascar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Madagascar" title="Education in Madagascar">Education in Madagascar</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Diego_Suarez_Antsiranana_urban_public_primary_school_(EPP)_Madagascar.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Students working in groups in classroom as teacher observes" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Diego_Suarez_Antsiranana_urban_public_primary_school_%28EPP%29_Madagascar.jpg/220px-Diego_Suarez_Antsiranana_urban_public_primary_school_%28EPP%29_Madagascar.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Diego_Suarez_Antsiranana_urban_public_primary_school_%28EPP%29_Madagascar.jpg/330px-Diego_Suarez_Antsiranana_urban_public_primary_school_%28EPP%29_Madagascar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Diego_Suarez_Antsiranana_urban_public_primary_school_%28EPP%29_Madagascar.jpg/440px-Diego_Suarez_Antsiranana_urban_public_primary_school_%28EPP%29_Madagascar.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2916" data-file-height="2386"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Diego_Suarez_Antsiranana_urban_public_primary_school_(EPP)_Madagascar.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Education_in_Madagascar" title="Education in Madagascar">Education</a> access and quality were prioritized under <a href="/wiki/Marc_Ravalomanana" title="Marc Ravalomanana">Ravalomanana</a>.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Prior to the 19th century, all education in Madagascar was informal and typically served to teach practical skills as well as social and cultural values, including respect for ancestors and elders.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_29-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-29">[29]</a></sup> The first formal European-style school was established in 1818 at <a href="/wiki/Toamasina" title="Toamasina">Toamasina</a> by members of the <a href="/wiki/London_Missionary_Society" title="London Missionary Society">London Missionary Society</a> (LMS). The LMS was invited by King Radama I to expand its schools throughout Imerina to teach basic literacy and numeracy to aristocratic children. The schools were closed by Ranavalona I in 1835<sup id="cite_ref-Ralibera_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ralibera-184">[184]</a></sup> but reopened and expanded in the decades after her death. </p>
      <p>By the end of the 19th century, Madagascar had the most developed and modern school system in pre-colonial Sub-Saharan Africa. Access to schooling was expanded in coastal areas during the colonial period, with French language and basic work skills becoming the focus of the curriculum. During the post-colonial First Republic, a continued reliance on French nationals as teachers, and French as the language of instruction, displeased those desiring a complete separation from the former colonial power.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_29-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-29">[29]</a></sup> Consequently, under the socialist Second Republic, French instructors and other nationals were expelled, Malagasy was declared the language of instruction, and a large cadre of young Malagasy were rapidly trained to teach at remote rural schools under the mandatory two-year national service policy.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>This policy, known as <i>malgachization</i>, coincided with a severe economic downturn and a dramatic decline in the quality of education. Those schooled during this period generally failed to master the French language or many other subjects and struggled to find employment, forcing many to take low-paying jobs in the informal or black market that mired them in deepening poverty. Excepting the brief presidency of Albert Zafy, from 1992 to 1996, Ratsiraka remained in power from 1975 to 2001 and failed to achieve significant improvements in education throughout his tenure.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Education was prioritized under the Ravalomanana administration (2002â09), and is currently free and compulsory from ages 6 to 13.<sup id="cite_ref-WBeducation_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WBeducation-187">[187]</a></sup> The primary schooling cycle is five years, followed by four years at the lower secondary level and three years at the upper secondary level.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_29-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-29">[29]</a></sup> During Ravalomanana's first term, thousands of new primary schools and additional classrooms were constructed, older buildings were renovated, and tens of thousands of new primary teachers were recruited and trained. Primary school fees were eliminated, and kits containing basic school supplies were distributed to primary students.<sup id="cite_ref-WBeducation_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WBeducation-187">[187]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Government school construction initiatives have ensured at least one primary school per <i>fokontany</i> and one lower secondary school within each commune. At least one upper secondary school is located in each of the larger urban centers.<sup id="cite_ref-INSTAT_148-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INSTAT-148">[148]</a></sup> The three branches of the national public university are located at Antananarivo, Mahajanga, and Fianarantsoa. These are complemented by public teacher-training colleges and several private universities and technical colleges.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_29-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-29">[29]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As a result of increased educational access, enrollment rates more than doubled between 1996 and 2006. However, education quality is weak, producing high rates of grade repetition and dropout.<sup id="cite_ref-WBeducation_187-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WBeducation-187">[187]</a></sup> Education policy in Ravalomanana's second term focused on quality issues, including an increase in minimum education standards for the recruitment of primary teachers from a middle school leaving certificate (BEPC) to a high school leaving certificate (BAC), and a reformed teacher training program to support the transition from traditional didactic instruction to student-centered teaching methods to boost student learning and participation in the classroom.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Public expenditure on education was 2.8&nbsp;percent of GDP in 2014. The literacy rate is estimated at 64.7%.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-24">[24]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Madagascar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Madagascar" title="Demographics of Madagascar">Demographics of Madagascar</a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Ethnic_groups_of_Madagascar_Map.png" class="image"><img alt="map of Madagascar showing distribution of Malagasy ethnic subgroups" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Ethnic_groups_of_Madagascar_Map.png/350px-Ethnic_groups_of_Madagascar_Map.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="445" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Ethnic_groups_of_Madagascar_Map.png 1.5x" data-file-width="471" data-file-height="599"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Ethnic_groups_of_Madagascar_Map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The regional distribution of <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_of_Madagascar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups of Madagascar">Malagasy ethnic subgroups</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>In 2018, the population of Madagascar was estimated at 26&nbsp;million, up from 2.2&nbsp;million in 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-189">[189]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_2019_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP_2019-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LOC_29-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-29">[29]</a></sup> The annual population growth rate in Madagascar was approximately 2.9&nbsp;percent in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-BGNote_23-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGNote-23">[23]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Approximately 42.5&nbsp;percent of the population is younger than 15 years of age, while 54.5&nbsp;percent are between the ages of 15 and 64. Those aged 65 and older form 3 percent of the total population.<sup id="cite_ref-AEO2011_160-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEO2011-160">[160]</a></sup> Only two general censuses, in 1975 and 1993, have been carried out after independence. The most densely populated regions of the island are the eastern highlands and the eastern coast, contrasting most dramatically with the sparsely populated western plains.<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_29-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-29">[29]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Madagascar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ethnic groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_of_Madagascar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups of Madagascar">Ethnic groups of Madagascar</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_people" title="Malagasy people">Malagasy</a> ethnic group forms over 90&nbsp;percent of Madagascar's population and is typically divided into 18 ethnic subgroups.<sup id="cite_ref-BGNote_23-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BGNote-23">[23]</a></sup> Recent DNA research revealed that the genetic makeup of the average Malagasy person constitutes an approximately equal blend of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asian</a> and East African genes,<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tofanelli_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tofanelli-192">[192]</a></sup> although the genetics of some communities show a predominance of Southeast Asian or East African origins or some Arab, <a href="/wiki/Indians_in_Madagascar" title="Indians in Madagascar">Indian</a>, or European ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-Adelaar_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adelaar-193">[193]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Southeast Asian features â specifically from the southern part of <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a> â are most predominant among the <a href="/wiki/Merina_people" title="Merina people">Merina</a> of the central highlands,<sup id="cite_ref-ethnicstrife_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ethnicstrife-136">[136]</a></sup> who form the largest Malagasy ethnic subgroup at approximately 26&nbsp;percent of the population, while certain communities among the coastal peoples (collectively called <i>cÃ´tiers</i>) have relatively stronger East African features. The largest coastal ethnic subgroups are the <a href="/wiki/Betsimisaraka_people" title="Betsimisaraka people">Betsimisaraka</a> (14.9&nbsp;percent) and the <a href="/wiki/Tsimihety" class="mw-redirect" title="Tsimihety">Tsimihety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sakalava" class="mw-redirect" title="Sakalava">Sakalava</a> (6&nbsp;percent each).<sup id="cite_ref-LOC_29-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LOC-29">[29]</a></sup> </p> 
      <table class="wikitable" style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto;"> 
       <tbody>
        <tr> 
         <th>Malagasy ethnic subgroups</th> 
         <th>Regional concentration </th>
        </tr> 
        <tr>

         <td>Former <a href="/wiki/Antsiranana_Province" title="Antsiranana Province">Antsiranana Province</a> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td><a href="/wiki/Sakalava_people" title="Sakalava people">Sakalava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vezo_people" title="Vezo people">Vezo</a></td> 
         <td>Former <a href="/wiki/Mahajanga_Province" title="Mahajanga Province">Mahajanga Province</a> </td>
        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td><a href="/wiki/Betsimisaraka_people" title="Betsimisaraka people">Betsimisaraka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sihanaka" title="Sihanaka">Sihanaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bezanozano" title="Bezanozano">Bezanozano</a></td>

        </tr> 
        <tr> 
         <td><a href="/wiki/Betsileo_people" title="Betsileo people">Betsileo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antaifasy" title="Antaifasy">Antaifasy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antambahoaka" title="Antambahoaka">Antambahoaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antemoro_people" title="Antemoro people">Antaimoro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antaisaka_people" title="Antaisaka people">Antaisaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanala" title="Tanala">Tanala</a></td>
